Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Necessity of Greek Logic
Ibn Taymiyya uses historical and legal arguments to reject the necessity of Greek logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument against the necessity of Greek logic by explaining how he uses analogies, prophetic examples, and the definition of bid'a to ground Islamic knowledge in revelation and prophetic example.

Core Idea
Effective action comes from prophetic command and familiarity, not Greek syllogisms.

Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Greek logic is not a wholesale rejection of reasoning but a refusal to grant it epistemological supremacy over revelation. In this passage, he weaves together analogies from warfare and language, historical examples from the Prophet's campaigns, and a precise legal definition of innovation (bid'a) to argue that Islamic knowledge is grounded in following Allah's commands and the Prophet's example, not in Aristotelian syllogistic forms. Understanding this critique will sharpen your ability to evaluate the role of rational methods in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim Ibn Taymiyya is attacking
Ibn Taymiyya is challenging the assertion that Greek logic is necessary for sound reasoning. His examples implicitly argue that practical knowledge and effective action can be attained without it.
Look for how he contrasts 'strength and courage' with 'similarity' (mujānasa) to make his point.
2
Analyze the analogy of mujānasa
He argues that familiarity with a people (mujānasa) gives one better understanding of them than abstract strength. The examples of the non-Arab imitating Arabs and vice versa illustrate that contextual knowledge surpasses formal reasoning.
The hadith about best/worst Arabs/non-Arabs reinforces the idea that imitation of the better model is praiseworthy.
3
Examine the prophetic examples
The Prophet used a mangonel at Ta'if and a trench at Khandaq—tactics not previously prescribed. These were accepted because they served commanded ends. This shows that means are validated by their ends, not by a fixed logical method.
Note the role of Salman's suggestion: it was accepted because it helped achieve the commanded goal.
4
Extract the definition of bid'a
Ibn Taymiyya defines bid'a as what Allah and His Messenger did not command (neither obligatory nor recommended). Actions commanded through legal evidence, even if done after the Prophet, are part of the religion (sunna).
This definition distinguishes between innovations (rejected) and actions based on command (accepted).
5
Connect the critique to Greek logic
The overall argument is that Islamic knowledge is derived from following commands and prophetic precedent, not from abstract syllogisms imported from the Greeks. Greek logic is neither necessary nor sufficient for correct reasoning in Islam.
Pay attention to how the definition of bid'a and the expansion of sunna serve to defend the Islamic legal tradition against claims of methodological insufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يكون الخبير بحروبهم أقدر على حربهم ممن ليس كذلك، لا لفضل قوته وشجاعته ولكن لمجانسته لهم، كما يكون الأعجمي المتشبه بالعرب ـ وهم خيارُ العجم - أعلم بمخاطبة قومه الأعاجم من العربي، وكما يكون العربي المتشبه بالعجم - وهم أدنى العرب - أعلم بمخاطبة العرب من العجمي، فقد جاء في الحديث: خيارُ عَجَمِكم المتشبهون بعَرَبكم، وشرارُ عربكم المتشبهون بعجمكم. ولهذا لما حاصر النبي الله أهل الطائف رماهم بالمَنْجَنِيق، وقاتلهم قتالا لم يقاتِل مثله في المُزَاحَفة في يوم بدر وغيره. وكذلك لمَّا حُوصر المسلمون عام الخندق اتخذوا من الخندق ما لم يحتاجوا إليه في غير الحصار. وقد قيل: إن سلمان أشار عليهم بذلك، فسلَّموا ذلك لأنه طريقٌ إلى فعل ما أمر الله به ورسوله. وقد قررنا في قاعدة السُّنة والبدعة أن البدعة في الدِّين هي ما لم يشرعه الله ورسوله، وهو ما لم يأمر به أمر إيجاب ولا استحباب. فأما ما أمر به أمر إيجاب أو استحباب، وعُلِمَ الأمرُ به بالأدلة الشرعية، فهو من الدين الذي شرعه الله، وإن تنازع أولو الأمر في بعض ذلك. وسواء كان هذا مفعولاً على عهد النبي له أو لم يكن. فما فُعِلَ بعده بأمره من قتال المرتدين والخوارج المارقين وفارس والروم والترك، وإخراج اليهود والنصارى من جزيرة العرب، وغير ذلك ـ هو من سُنّته. ولهذا كان عمر بن عبد العزيز يقول : سَنَّ رسول الله وولاة الأمر بعده سُنَنا الأخذُ بها تصديق لكتاب الله، واستكمال لطاعة الله، وقوَّةً على دين الله، ليس لأحدٍ تغييرها ولا النظرُ في رأي من خالفها، من أهتدى بها فهو مهتد، ومن استنصر بها فهو منصور، ومن خالفها واتَّبع غير سبيل المؤمنين ولاه الله ما تولى وأصلاه جهنم وساءت مصيرا.
And the expert in their wars may be more capable of waging war against them than one who is not, not because of his strength and courage but due to his similarity to them. Just as the non-Arab who imitates the Arabs—and they are the best of non-Arabs—is more knowledgeable in addressing his fellow non-Arabs than the Arab, and just as the Arab who imitates the non-Arabs—and they are the worst of Arabs—is more knowledgeable in addressing the Arabs than the non-Arab. It has come in the hadith: 'The best of your non-Arabs are those who imitate your Arabs, and the worst of your Arabs are those who imitate your non-Arabs.' For this reason, when the Prophet (peace be upon him) besieged the people of Ta'if, he shot them with the mangonel and fought them with a fighting he did not fight like in the advance [muzahafa] on the day of Badr and elsewhere. Likewise, when the Muslims were besieged in the year of the Trench, they took to the trench what they did not need in other than siege. And it is said: Salman suggested that to them, so they accepted it because it is a way to do what Allah and His Messenger commanded. And we have established in the principle of sunna and innovation that innovation in religion is that which Allah and His Messenger have not legislated, and that is that which He has not commanded as an obligation or recommendation. As for that which He has commanded as an obligation or recommendation, and the command for it is known through legal evidence, it is from the religion that Allah has legislated, even if those in authority disagree about some of it. And it is the same whether this was done during the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) or not. For what was done after his time by his command, such as fighting the apostates, the rebellious Kharijites, Persia, Rome, and the Turks, and expelling the Jews and Christians from the Arabian Peninsula, and other things—that is from his sunna. For this reason, 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz used to say: 'The Messenger of Allah and the rulers after him established sunnas; adhering to them is a confirmation of the Book of Allah, a completion of obedience to Allah, and a strength for the religion of Allah. No one has the right to change them or to consider the opinion of those who contradict them. Whoever is guided by them is rightly guided, whoever seeks victory through them is victorious, and whoever opposes them and follows other than the path of the believers, Allah will turn him to what he has chosen and burn him in Hell, and evil is the destination.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is part of Ibn Taymiyya's critical argument against the necessity of Greek logic. He uses historical examples to demonstrate that practical knowledge and effective action come from familiarity and following the Prophet's example, not from abstract syllogistic reasoning. First, he gives an analogy: an expert in a particular people's warfare is more effective because of similarity (mujānasa), not superior strength. Similarly, a non-Arab who imitates Arabs is better at addressing non-Arabs than an Arab, and vice versa. He cites a hadith about the best and worst of each group. Then he applies this to the Prophet's military tactics: the Prophet used a mangonel at Ta'if and dug a trench at the Battle of the Trench—methods not previously prescribed but effective and accepted because they served the commanded goal. The suggestion by Salman was accepted. Ibn Taymiyya then shifts to legal theory: he defines bid'a (innovation) as what Allah and His Messenger did not command, distinguishing between commanded obligations/recommendations (which are part of the religion) and innovations. Actions done after the Prophet's time by his command (like fighting apostates, etc.) are part of his sunna. He quotes 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz affirming that the sunnas established by the Prophet and caliphs are authoritative and must be followed. The overall point is that Islamic law and practice are based on following revealed commands and prophetic example, not on rationalist logic imported from the Greeks.

Key Terms
بدعة
سنة
مجانسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya's argument in this passage, why was the Prophet's use of the mangonel at Ta'if and the trench at Khandaq valid?
A) Because they were commanded by direct revelation
B) Because they were effective means to achieve a commanded end
C) Because the Prophet learned them from the Greeks
D) Because Salman was a Persian and knew siege warfare

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that effective action and correct knowledge come from following prophetic command and familiarity, not from Greek syllogistic logic; he defines innovation as what is not commanded, thereby grounding Islamic practice in revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's definition of bid'a, analyze whether the following action is an innovation: 'Organizing a charity fundraiser using modern marketing techniques that were not used by the Prophet.' Provide reasoning based on the passage.
?
Compare the analogy of mujānasa with the concept of 'illa (effective cause) in usul al-fiqh. How does similarity in understanding relate to the criteria for extending a legal ruling through qiyas?

Refuting the Misidentification of Angels with Celestial Intellects
Author refutes philosophers' claim that celestial intellects and souls are angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scriptural definition of angels as God's messengers and contrast it with the philosophers' reinterpretation as mere intellects and souls, evaluating the author's grounds for rejection.

Core Idea
Angels are God's messengers, not disembodied intellects — a scriptural refutation of philosophical reinterpretation.

The philosophers, seeking to reconcile revelation with Aristotelian cosmology, identified their celestial intellects and souls with the angels of scripture. In this passage, the author dismantles that equation by returning to the Qur'anic and prophetic definition: angels are fundamentally messengers (rusul) of God, a role that encompasses both cosmic administration and prophetic revelation. By analyzing his scriptural proofs, you will learn how to distinguish a revealed concept from a philosophical abstraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate where the author describes the philosophers' assertion that 'the intellects and souls are [what is meant by] angels in the speech of the prophets.' Note that they claim to be reconciling philosophy and religion.
The philosophers here include figures like Ibn Rushd (Averroes) and Al-Fārābī.
2
Distinguish agreement from disagreement
The author acknowledges that the philosophers' idea of 'spirits in the heavens' resembles scriptural teachings, but he strongly denies that these are the same as angels. Mark the phrase 'they are not the angels'.
This shows the author does not reject all philosophical cosmology, only the equation.
3
Extract the scriptural definition of angels
From the Quranic verses cited, deduce the definition: angels are 'messengers' (rusul) of God. List the two domains of their message: cosmic command (administering the universe) and religious command (bringing revelation).
Verses: Fāṭir 35:1, al-Anʿām 6:61, al-Zukhruf 43:80, al-Naḥl 16:2, al-Shūrā 42:51.
4
Compare with the philosophical concept
Contrast the scriptural definition with the philosophers' 'intellects and souls'. The latter are purely metaphysical, necessary emanations without volition or mission. The author says they 'resemble them in some respects' but are not identical.
The resemblance might be in their celestial dwelling, but not in their function.
5
Assess the author's grounds for refutation
Identify the type of evidence the author uses: scriptural (Qur'an and hadith) and linguistic (the name 'angel' implies messenger). Consider why this is a valid critique from the perspective of Islamic theology.
This is an epistemological critique: revelation defines terms, not Greek philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم: أما إثباتكم أن في السماء أرواحا فهذا يُشْبِهُ ما في القرآن وغيره من كتب الله، ولكن ليست هي الملائكة كما يقول الذين يزعمون منكم أنهم آمنوا بما أُنزِلَ على الرسول وما أُنزِل من قبله ويقولون: ما أردنا إلا الإحسان والتوفيق بين الشَّريعة والفلسفة، فإنهم قالوا: العقول والنفوس عند الملائكة عند الأنبياء. وليس كذلك، لكن تُشْبِهُها من بعض الوجوه. هي فإن أسم الملائكة والمَلَك يتضمَّنُ أنهم رُسُلُ الله، كما قال تعالى: ﴿جَاعِلِ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا﴾ [فاطر: ١]، وكما قال: ﴿وَالْمُرْسَلَاتِ عُرْفًا﴾ [المرسلات: ١]، فالملائكة رسل الله في تنفيذ أمره الكوني الذي يدبر به السموات والأرض، كما قال: ﴿حَتَّى إِذَا جَاءَ أَحَدَكُمُ الْمَوْتُ تَوَفَّتْهُ رُسُلُنَا وَهُمْ لَا يُفَرِّطُونَ﴾ [الأنعام: ٦١]، وكما قال: ﴿بَلَى وَرُسُلُنَا لَدَيْهِمْ يَكْتُبُونَ﴾ [الزخرف: ٨٠]، وأمره الديني الذي تَنْزِلُ به الملائكة، فإنه يُنَزِّلُ الْمَلَائِكَةَ بِالرُّوحِ مِنْ أَمْرِهِ، عَلَى مَن يَشَاءُ مِنْ عِبَادِهِ [النحل: ٢] وقال: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لِبَشَرٍ أَن يُكَلِّمَهُ اللَّهُ إِلَّا وَحْيًا أَوْ مِن وَرَاءِ حِجَابٍ أَوْ يُرْسِلَ رَسُولًا فَيُوحِيَ بِإِذْنِهِ مَا يَشَاءُ إِنَّهُ عَلِيٌّ حَكِيمٌ﴾ [الشورى: ٥١]، وقال: ﴿اللَّهُ يَصْطَفِي مِنَ الْمَلَائِكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِنَ النَّاسِ﴾ [الحج: ٧٥]. وملائكة الله لا يحصي عددهم إلا الله، كما قال تعالى: ﴿وَمَا جَعَلْنَا أَصْحَابَ النَّارِ إِلَّا مَلَائِكَةً وَمَا جَعَلْنَا عِدَّتَهُمْ إِلَّا فِتْنَةً لِلَّذِينَ كَفَرُوا لِيَسْتَيْقِنَ الَّذِينَ أُوتُوا الْكِتَابَ وَيَزْدَادَ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِيمَانًا وَلَا يَرْتَابَ الَّذِينَ أُوتُوا الْكِتَابَ وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ وَلِيَقُولَ الَّذِينَ فِي قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌ وَالْكَافِرُونَ مَاذَا أَرَادَ اللَّهُ بِهَذَا مَثَلًا كَذَلِكَ يُضِلُّ اللَّهُ مَن يَشَاءُ وَيَهْدِي مَن يَشَاءُ وَمَا يَعْلَمُ جُنُودَ رَبِّكَ إِلَّا هُوَ﴾ [المدثر: ٣١]. وقيل لهم: الذي في الكتاب والسنة مِن ذِكْر الملائكة وكثرتهم أمر لا يُخصر، حتى قال النبيُّ ﷺ: أَطَّت السماءُ وحُقَّ لها أن تنط، ما فيها موضع أربع أصابع إلا ملك قائم أو قاعد أو راكع أو ساجد.
It was said to them: As for your assertion that there are spirits in the heavens, this resembles what is in the Quran and other books of God, but they are not the angels as claimed by those among you who pretend that they believe in what was revealed to the Messenger and what was revealed before him, and they say: 'We only intended beneficence and reconciliation between the Sacred Law and philosophy.' For they said: 'The intellects and souls are [what is meant by] angels in the speech of the prophets.' It is not so, but they resemble them in some respects. For the name 'angels' (al-malā'ika) and 'angel' (malak) implies that they are messengers of God, as He said: 'Who made the angels messengers' [Fāṭir:1], and as He said: 'By those sent forth in succession' [al-Mursalāt:1]. So the angels are God's messengers in executing His cosmic command by which He administers the heavens and the earth, as He said: 'Until when death comes to one of you, Our messengers take him, and they do not fail' [al-Anʿām:61], and as He said: 'Yes, and Our messengers are with them recording' [al-Zukhruf:80]; and His religious command with which the angels descend, for He sends down the angels with the Spirit by His command upon whom He wills of His servants [al-Naḥl:2]; and He said: 'It is not for any human that God should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil or by sending a messenger who reveals by His permission what He wills, indeed He is Exalted, Wise' [al-Shūrā:51]; and He said: 'God chooses messengers from the angels and from mankind' [al-Ḥajj:75]. And none can count the angels of God except God, as He said: 'And We have not made the keepers of the Fire except angels, and We have not made their number except as a trial for those who disbelieve, so that those who were given the Scripture may be certain and those who believe may increase in faith, and those who were given the Scripture and the believers may not doubt, and so that those in whose hearts is sickness and the disbelievers may say: What did God intend by this example? Thus God leads astray whom He wills and guides whom He wills. And none knows the soldiers of your Lord except Him' [al-Muddaththir:31]. And it was said to them: What is in the Book and the Sunnah regarding the mention of angels and their multitude is something beyond count, until the Prophet ﷺ said: 'The heaven creaked, and it has the right to creak; there is no space of four fingers in it except that an angel is standing, sitting, bowing, or prostrating.'
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers who identify their 'intellects and souls' (al-ʿuqūl wa al-nufūs) with the angels mentioned in revelation. The author begins by acknowledging that the philosophers' claim of spirits in the heavens does resemble what is in the Quran and other scriptures, but he firmly denies that these spirits are the angels. He targets those who claim to reconcile philosophy and religion, such as Ibn Rushd and others, who assert that the 'intellects and souls' correspond to the angels spoken of by the prophets. The author then proceeds to define angels properly: the very term 'angel' (malak) entails that they are messengers (rusul) of God. He supports this with Quranic verses: Fāṭir 35:1 calls angels 'messengers', and al-Mursalāt 77:1 swears by the sent ones. Angels are God's messengers in two domains: His cosmic command (al-amr al-kawnī), by which He administers the heavens and the earth—citing al-Anʿām 6:61 about the angels of death, and al-Zukhruf 43:80 about recording angels; and His religious command (al-amr al-dīnī), by which angels descend with revelation—citing al-Naḥl 16:2 and al-Shūrā 42:51, which describe revelation through an angelic messenger. The author also emphasizes the vast multitude of angels, quoting al-Muddaththir 74:31 that none but God knows the number of His soldiers, and a hadith where the Prophet describes the heavens filled with angels in various postures. The overall argument is that the philosophers' redefinition of angels is a distortion; the true nature of angels as God's messengers is rooted in scripture and tradition.

Key Terms
الملائكة
الرسل
العقول والنفوس

Key Takeaway
Angels are defined scripturally as God's messengers, a role that cannot be reduced to the philosophers' purely metaphysical intellects and souls.

Refuting the Philosophers' Identification of Angels with Intellects and Souls
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the philosophers' equation of angels with intellects and souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the philosophers' claim about angels and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique using revelation-based reasoning.

Core Idea
The philosophers reduce angels to abstract intellects; Ibn Taymiyya restores their full Qur'anic reality.

The philosophers of Islam, drawing on Neoplatonic cosmology, claimed that the angels are the 'intellects' and 'souls' of the celestial spheres. They even identified the nineteen angels of Hell mentioned in the Qur'an with these abstract entities. In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp refutation, arguing that this identification is based on ignorance of revelation and reduces the rich, active role of angels as described in the Qur'an to a mere shadow. Let us analyse his critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Locate the philosophers' claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the philosophers make the angels 'ten' or 'nineteen' and claim they are 'the intellects and souls' (العقول والنفوس). This is the target of his critique.
The 'ten' refers to the ten intellects in the Neoplatonic emanation scheme; the 'nineteen' refers to the angels over Hell (Surah Al-Muddaththir: 30).
2
Understand the objection: mismatch of names
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the names 'angel' and 'intellect/soul' do not agree in attribute or measure. They only share the general notion of being a spirit attached to the heavens. This is insufficient for identification.
He uses the analogy of synonyms (الألفاظ المترادفة) to show that mere conceptual overlap does not prove identity.
3
Extract the charge of reduction
Ibn Taymiyya says the philosophers only attribute 'mere knowledge for the intellects, and volitional movement for the souls.' This is a tiny fraction of what angels truly are. He compares their belief to that of the Samaritans who accepted only a few prophets.
4
Examine the Qur'anic counter-evidence
Ibn Taymiyya cites numerous verses showing that angels have multiple attributes: continuous glorification, prostration, intercession, carrying the Throne, descending as armies, etc. These go far beyond mere knowledge and movement.
List at least five attributes from the verses he quotes.
5
Assess the validity of the critique
Ibn Taymiyya's critique is based on the principle that revealed descriptions must be taken fully, not selectively. The philosophers' failure to account for these verses shows their claim is based on ignorance.

تعالى: ﴿ تَكَادُ السَّمَوَاتُ يَتَفَطَّرْنَ مِن فَوْقِهِنَّ وَالْمَلَيْكَةُ يُسَبِّحُونَ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّهِمْ وَيَسْتَغْفِرُونَ لِمَن فِي الْأَرْضِ أَلَا إِنَّ اللَّهَ هُوَ الْغَفُورُ الرَّحِيمُ ) [الشورى: ٥]. فمن جعلهم عشرة، أو تسعة عشر، أو زعم أن التسعة عشر الذين على سَقَر هم العقول والنفوس = فهذا جهله بما جاء عن الله ورسوله وضلاله في ذلك بيّن؛ إذ لم يتفق الاسمان في صفة المسمى ولا في قدره كما تكونُ الألفاظ المترادفة، وإنما أتفق المسمَّيان في كون كل منهما روحًا متعلقًا بالسَّماوات، وهذا من بعض صفات ملائكة السماوات. فالذي أثبتوه بعض الصفات لبعض الملائكة، وهو بالنسبة إلى الملائكة وصفاتهم وأقدارهم وأعدادهم في غاية القِلَّة، أقل مما يؤمن به السَّامِرَة من الأنبياء بالنسبة إلى الأنبياء؛ إذ لا يؤمنون بعد موسى ويوشع بنبي. كيف وهم لم يثبتوا للملائكة من الصفة إلا مجرَّدَ ما عَلِمُوه من نفوسهم، مجرَّد العلم للعقول، والحركة الإرادية للنفوس. ومن المعلوم أن الملائكة لهم من العلوم والأحوال والإرادات والأعمال ما لا يحصيه إلا ذو الجلال، ووصفهم في القرآن بالتسبيح والعبادة الله أكثر من أن يُذكر هنا، كما ذكر [تعالى] في خطابه للملائكة، وأمره لهم بالسجود لآدم، وقوله : ( فإنِ اسْتَكْبَرُوا فَالَّذِينَ عِندَ رَبِّكَ يُسَبِّحُونَ لَهُ بِالَّيْلِ وَالنَّهَارِ وَهُمْ لَا يَسْمُونَ ﴾ [فصلت: ۱۳۸، وقوله: ﴿ إِنَّ الَّذِينَ عِندَ رَبِّكَ لَا يَسْتَكْبِرُونَ عَنْ عِبَادَتِهِ وَيُسَبِّحُونَهُ، وَلَهُ يَسْجُدُونَ ﴾ [الأعراف: ٢٠٦]، وقوله: ﴿ وَقَالُوا اتَّخَذَ الرَّحْمَنُ وَلَدا سُبْحَنَه بَلْ عِبَادٌ تُكْرَمُونَ ) لَا يَسْبِقُونَهُ بِالْقَوْلِ وَهُم بِأَمْرِه يَعْمَلُونَ يَعْلَمُ مَا بَيْنَ أَيْدِيهِمْ وَمَا خَلْفَهُمْ وَلَا يَشْفَعُونَ إِلَّا لِمَنِ ارْتَضَى وَهُم مِنْ خَشْيَتِهِ، مُشْفِقُونَ * وَمَن يَقُل مِنْهُمْ إِنّى إِلَهُ مِن دُونِهِ، فَذَلِكَ نَجْزِيهِ جَهَنَّه كَذَلِكَ نَجْزِي الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٦ - ٢٩]، وقوله: ﴿ اللهُ يَصْطَفى مِنَ الْمَلَيكَةِ رُسُلًا وَمِنَ النَّاسِ ﴾ [الحج: ٧٥]، وقوله: ﴿ الَّذِينَ يَحْمِلُونَ الْعَرْشَ وَمَنْ حَوْلَهُ يُسَبِّحُونَ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّهِمْ وَيُؤْمِنُونَ بِهِ، وَيَسْتَغْفِرُونَ لِلَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا ) الآية [غافر: ٢٧]، وقوله: ﴿وَالْمَلَيْكَةُ يُسَبِّحُونَ بِحَمْدِ رَبِّهِمْ وَيَسْتَغْفِرُونَ لِمَن فِي الْأَرْضِ ﴾ [الشورى: ٥]، وقوله: كُلٌّ آمَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَمَلَيكَيهِ، وَكَبِهِ وَرُسُلِهِ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٥] وقوله: ﴿إِذْ تَقُولُ لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ أَلَن يَكْفِيكُمْ أَن يُمِدَّكُمْ رَبِّكُم بِثَلَاثَةِ وَالْفِ مِنَ الْمَلَتَيكَةِ مُنزَلِينَ بَلَى إِن تَصْبِرُوا وَتَتَّقُوا وَيَأْتُوكُم مِّن فَورِهِمْ هَذَا يُمْدِدْكُمْ رَبُّكُم بِخَمْسَةِ وَالفِ مِنَ الْمَلَتَيكَةِ مُسَوّمِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران : ١٢٤، ١٢٥)، وقوله: ﴿إِذْ يُوحَى رَبُّكَ إِلَى الْمَلَكَةِ أَنِي مَعَكُمْ فَتَبْتُوا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا ﴾ [الأنفال: ١٢]، وقوله: ﴿ ثُمَّ أَنزَلَ الله سَكِينَتَهُ عَلَى رَسُولِهِ، وَعَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَأَنزَلَ جُنُودًا لَوْ تَرَوْهَا ﴾ [التوبة: ٢٦]
The Almighty said: 'The heavens are almost rent from above them, and the angels glorify the praise of their Lord and ask forgiveness for those on earth; verily Allah is the Oft-Forgiving, Most Merciful.' [Surah Ash-Shura: 5]. So whoever makes them ten, or nineteen, or claims that the nineteen who are over Hell are the intellects and souls – this is his ignorance of what came from Allah and His Messenger, and his misguidance in that is clear; because the two names do not agree in the attribute of the named nor in its measure, as synonymous words are, but the two named things agree only in that each is a spirit attached to the heavens – and this is some of the attributes of the angels of the heavens. So what they have established is some attributes of some angels, and that is, in relation to the angels and their attributes, their measures, and their numbers, extremely little – less than what the Samaritans believe regarding the prophets in relation to the prophets, since they do not believe in any prophet after Moses and Joshua. How so, when they have not established for the angels any attribute except merely what they knew from themselves: mere knowledge for the intellects, and volitional movement for the souls. And it is known that the angels have sciences, states, wills, and deeds that none can enumerate except the Lord of Majesty, and their description in the Qur'an of glorification and worship of Allah is more than can be mentioned here, as [the Almighty] mentioned in His address to the angels, and His command to them to prostrate to Adam, and His saying: 'But if they are arrogant, then those who are with your Lord glorify Him by night and day, and they do not tire.' [Surah Fussilat: 38], and His saying: 'Indeed, those who are with your Lord are not arrogant in worship of Him, and they glorify Him, and to Him they prostrate.' [Surah Al-A'raf: 206], and His saying: 'And they said, "The Most Merciful has taken a son." Exalted is He! Rather, they are honored servants. They do not precede Him in speech, and they act by His command. He knows what is before them and what is behind them, and they intercede only for whom He approves, and they are in awe of Him. And whoever of them says, "Indeed, I am a god besides Him," that one We shall reward with Hell. Thus do We reward the wrongdoers.' [Surah Al-Anbiya': 26-29], and His saying: 'Allah chooses messengers from the angels and from the people.' [Surah Al-Hajj: 75], and His saying: 'Those who bear the Throne and those around it glorify the praise of their Lord and believe in Him and ask forgiveness for those who have believed.' [Surah Ghafir: 7], and His saying: 'And the angels glorify the praise of their Lord and ask forgiveness for those on earth.' [Surah Ash-Shura: 5], and His saying: 'Each one believes in Allah, His angels, His books, and His messengers.' [Surah Al-Baqarah: 285], and His saying: 'When you said to the believers, "Is it not sufficient for you that your Lord should reinforce you with three thousand angels sent down?" ... [Yes], if you are patient and righteous and they [i.e., the enemy] come upon you immediately, your Lord will reinforce you with five thousand angels having marks [of distinction].' [Surah Aal-e-Imran: 124-125], and His saying: 'When your Lord inspired to the angels, "Indeed, I am with you, so strengthen those who have believed."' [Surah Al-Anfal: 12], and His saying: 'Then Allah sent down His tranquility upon His Messenger and upon the believers and sent down forces you did not see.' [Surah At-Tawbah: 26]
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' doctrine that identifies the angels (specifically the 'ten intellects' or the 'nineteen' mentioned in the Qur'an as guardians of Hell) with the intellects and souls of the celestial spheres. Ibn Taymiyya begins by quoting the Qur'anic verse (Ash-Shura: 5) which describes angels as glorifying God and seeking forgiveness, establishing their active, personal worship. He then attacks the claim that angels are merely 'ten' or 'nineteen' intellects and souls, arguing that this is pure ignorance of revelation. The identification fails because the names 'angel' and 'intellect/soul' do not share the same attributes or measure; they only share the general notion of being a spirit attached to the heavens. What the philosophers have affirmed is a tiny fraction of the true nature of angels, comparable to the Samaritans' belief in only a few prophets. The philosophers only attribute to angels what they derive from their own souls: knowledge (for intellects) and volitional movement (for souls). But the Qur'an presents angels with far more extensive knowledge, states, wills, and deeds beyond human enumeration. Ibn Taymiyya then marshals numerous verses to demonstrate the full scope of angelic attributes: they glorify God continuously, prostrate to Adam, intercede, carry the Throne, are messengers, descend with armies, and are sent to aid believers. These descriptions contradict the reduction of angels to mere abstract intellects and souls. Thus, the philosophers' claim is not only insufficient but fundamentally misguided, based on ignoring the detailed revelation about angels.

التسعة عشر
الملائكة

The First Falsehood: Criticizing with Non-Revealed Terms
Author critiques the opponent's use of non-revealed blame terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a term of praise or blame lacks revelatory basis and apply Ibn Taymiyya's two-step method (clarification and proof) to evaluate such terms.

Core Idea
Praise and blame in religion must be based on revelation, not invented terms.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya exposes a key fallacy in theological polemics: using terms like 'hashw,' 'tashbih,' and 'tajsim' to condemn opponents without any scriptural authority. He insists that all terms of praise or blame in religious discourse must originate from the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus. He also provides a methodology for testing such terms: clarify their meaning and then prove the blame from Shari'a. This lesson will equip you to apply Ibn Taymiyya's critical standard to any theological accusation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms of blame
Find the three terms the opponent used: hashw, tashbih, tajsim. These are the target of Ibn Taymiyya's critique.
These terms are used to blame certain groups. Note which group is being accused.
2
Check scriptural basis
Determine if each term appears in the Quran, hadith, or the usage of the Salaf. Ibn Taymiyya says none are in revelation; only tashbih has some limited precedent.
Use the examples he provides: believer, disbeliever, scholar, etc. – these are legitimate.
3
Apply the two-step method
For any such term, ask: (a) What does it mean? (b) Does Shari'a explicitly blame those characterized by it? Ibn Taymiyya shows the opponent fails both steps.
This is the core of his methodological critique.
4
Evaluate the opponent's alternative
If the opponent had used terms that God Himself negates (like kufu, nidd, sami), his criticism would have some basis. However, he would still need to prove that the accused actually has that attribute.
This demonstrates that even scriptural terms require careful application.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود أن كلامه فيه حق. وفيه من الباطل أمور:
أحدها: قوله: «لا يتحاشى من الحشو [والتشبيه] والتجسيم» ذم للناس بأسماء ما أنزل الله بها من سلطان، والذي مدحه زَيْن ودمه شَيْن هو الله. والأسماء التي يتعلَّق بها المدح والذم من الدين لا تكون إلا من الأسماء التي أنزل الله بها سلطانه ودلّ عليها الكتاب والسنة أو الإجماع، كالمؤمن والكافر، والعالم والجاهل، والمقتصد، والملحد.
فأما هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة فليست في كتاب الله، ولا في حديث عن رسول الله، ولا نطق بها أحدٌ من سلف الأمة وأئمتها لا نفيا ولا إثباتًا، وأول من ابتدع الذم بها المعتزلة الذين فارقوا جماعة المسلمين، فاتباع سبيل المعتزلة دون سبيل سلف الأمة تركُ للقول السديد الواجب في الدين، واتباع لسبيل المبتدعة الضالين.
وليس فيها ما يوجد عن بعض السَّلف ذمُّه إلا لفظ «التشبيه»، فلو أقتصر عليه لكان له قدوة من السلف الصالح، ولو أنه ذكر الأسماء التي نفاها الله في القرآن مثل لفظ «الكُفؤ، والند، والسَّمِي»، وقال: «منهم من لا يتحاشى من التمثيل» ونحوه = لكان قد ذَمَّ بقول نفاه الله في كتابه، ودلّ القرآن على ذمّ قائله، ثم يُنظَر: هل قائله موصوفٌ بما وصفه به من الذم أم لا؟
فأما الأسماء التي لم يَدُلُّ الشَّرعُ على ذمّ أهلها ولا مدحهم، فيُحْتَاجُ فيها إلى مقامين:
أحدهما: بيان المراد بها.
والثاني: بيان أن أولئك مذمومون في الشريعة.
والمعترض عليه له أن يمنع المقامين، فيقول: لا تُسَلَّمُ أن الذين عنيتهم داخلون في هذه الأسماء التي ذَمَمْتَها، ولم يَقُم دليل شرعي على ذمها، وإن دخلوا فيها فلا نُسَلِّمُ أن كلَّ من دخل في هذه الأسماء فهو مذموم في الشرع.
And the intended meaning is that his speech contains truth. And in it there are falsehoods: The first: his statement: 'He does not shy away from redundancy [and anthropomorphism] and corporealism' — a criticism of people with names which God has sent down no authority for. And the One whom He praises is adorned and whom He blames is disgraced by God. And the names that are attached to praise and blame in religion are only those names which God has sent down His authority for and which the Book, the Sunnah, or consensus have indicated, such as the believer and the disbeliever, the scholar and the ignorant, the moderate and the heretic. As for these three expressions, they are not in the Book of God, nor in any hadith from the Messenger of God, nor did any of the predecessors of the nation and its imams utter them, either in negation or affirmation. And the first to innovate blame with them were the Mu'tazila who separated from the community of Muslims. So following the way of the Mu'tazila instead of the way of the predecessors of the nation is abandonment of the sound statement obligatory in religion, and following the way of the misguided innovators. And among them there is nothing for which blame is found from some of the predecessors except the word 'tashbih'. So if he had restricted himself to it, he would have had a model from the pious predecessors. And if he had mentioned the names that God negated in the Quran, such as the word 'al-kufu', 'al-nidd', and 'al-sami', and said: 'Among them are those who do not shy away from tashbih' and the like, then he would have criticized with a statement that God negated in His Book, and the Quran indicates the blame of its speaker. Then it is to be examined: is its speaker described with the blame he described him with or not? As for the names which the Law has not indicated the blame of their people nor their praise, two matters are needed concerning them: First: clarifying what is meant by them. Second: establishing that those people are blameworthy in the Shari'a. And the one objected to has the right to block both matters, saying: It is not granted that those you mean are included in these names which you have blamed them with, and no legal proof has been established for their blame. And even if they are included, we do not grant that everyone included in these names is blameworthy in the Shari'a.
Source pages p. 209

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of a claim made by someone (likely a theologian) who used the terms 'redundancy', 'anthropomorphism', and 'corporealism' to blame certain people. Ibn Taymiyya begins by acknowledging that the speech contains some truth. However, he then identifies falsehoods. The first falsehood is the use of these three terms as instruments of blame. Ibn Taymiyya argues that in religious matters, terms of praise or blame must have a basis in revelation: they must be found in the Quran, the Sunnah, or the consensus of the early community (Salaf). He gives examples like believer, disbeliever, scholar, ignorant. The three terms (hashw, tashbih, tajsim) are not found in any revealed source, nor were they used by the Salaf. They were first innovated by the Mu'tazila, who deviated from the Muslim community. Following their path is abandoning the correct speech required in religion. Ibn Taymiyya notes that among these three, only 'tashbih' has some precedent among the Salaf as a term of blame. He suggests that if the opponent had only used that term, or had used terms that God Himself negated in the Quran (like 'kufu', 'nidd', 'sami'), then his criticism would have a scriptural basis. But the issue is not just using the term; one must also establish that the person criticized is actually described by that blameworthy attribute. Ibn Taymiyya then discusses a methodological point: for names not mentioned in revelation as praiseworthy or blameworthy, two things are required: (1) clarification of meaning, and (2) proof that those people are blameworthy in the Shari'a. The person being objected to can block both: he can deny that the intended people fall under these terms, or deny that a legal proof exists for their blame, or even if they fall under them, deny that everyone under those terms is blameworthy. This passage exemplifies Ibn Taymiyya's empirical and revelatory approach: he insists that logical critique must be grounded in transmitted sources, not arbitrary nomenclature.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that theological accusations must be grounded in revealed terminology and subject to verification of meaning and legal blame.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine a common accusation in your own theological context (e.g., 'anthropomorphism' against Atharis). Apply Ibn Taymiyya's two-step method: (1) Does the term have a clear meaning? (2) Is there a clear legal (shar'i) basis for blaming someone who holds that specific belief? Write your analysis.
?
Identify a term of praise or blame used in a contemporary Islamic debate (e.g., 'extremist' or 'moderate'). Check whether it has a basis in the Quran, Sunnah, or consensus. If not, propose alternative terms that do.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique: Innovation is Abandoning the Salaf
Author argues that adherence to the Salaf is a sign of Sunnism, not innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument distinguishing the true hallmark of innovation from the false claim that following the Salaf is innovation.

Core Idea
Innovation is abandoning the Salaf, not claiming to follow them.

Ibn Taymiyya here performs a critical refinement: he corrects a common misconception that veneration of the Salaf is a mark of innovation. By analyzing which groups are actually accused of innovation and why, he establishes the opposite—that innovation consists precisely in departing from the Salaf. This passage sharpens our ability to distinguish between authentic Sunni identity and the polemical labeling of opponents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups accused of innovation
Find the sects Ibn Taymiyya mentions as commonly labeled innovators. He singles out the Rafidah as the most famous. Note the reasoning: they contradict hadith and Quran and attack the Salaf.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's definition of innovation's hallmark
Locate the sentence: 'the hallmark of the people of innovation is abandoning the adoption of following the Salaf'. This is his positive claim.
3
Analyze the counterclaim he refutes
He rejects the idea that adopting the Salaf is a hallmark of innovators. Understand why he calls this 'absolutely false'—it only arises where ignorance abounds.
4
Evaluate his evidence and reasoning
He uses the example of the Rafidah as empirical evidence and quotes Imam Ahmad to anchor his position in tradition. Assess whether his argument is internally consistent.

فالمقصود هنا أن المشهورين من الطوائف بين أهل السنة والجماعة العامة بالبدعة ليسوا منتحلين للسَّلف، بل أشهر الطوائف بالبدعة الرافضة، حتى إن العامة لا تعرفُ من شعائر البدع إلا الرفض، والسُّني في اصطلاحهم من لا يكون رافضيا؛ وذلك أنهم أكثر مخالفة للأحاديث النبوية ولمعاني القرآن، وأكثر قدحًا في سلف الأمة وأئمتها، وطعنا في جمهور الأمة من جميع الطوائف، فلمّا كانوا أبعد عن متابعة السلف كانوا أشهر بالبدعة. فعلِمَ أن شعار أهل البدع هو ترك انتحال أتباع السلف؛ ولهذا قال الإمام أحمد في رسالة عَبْدُوس بن مالك: «أصول السُّنة عندنا التمسك بما كان عليه أصحاب النبي ». وأما متكلمة أهل الإثبات من الكلابية والكَرَّامية والأشعرية، مع الفقهاء والصُّوفية وأهل الحديث، فهؤلاء في الجملة لا يطعنون في السلف، بل قد يوافقونهم في أكثر جُمَل مقالاتهم، لكنْ كلُّ من كان بالحديث من هؤلاء أعلم كان بمذهب السَّلف أعلم وله أتبع، وإنما يوجد تعظيمُ السَّلف عند طائفة بقدر استنانها وقلة ابتداعها. أما أن يكون أنتحالُ السَّلف من شعائر أهل البدع فهذا باطل قطعا؛ فإن ذلك غير ممكن إلا حيث يكثر الجهل ويقل العلم.
The intended point here is that those well-known among the sects of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a generally for innovation are not adherents of the Salaf, rather the most famous sect for innovation is the Rafidah, to the extent that the common people do not recognize any insignia of innovation except Rafd, and 'Sunni' in their parlance is one who is not a Rafidi. That is because they are the most contradictory to the prophetic hadiths and the meanings of the Quran, and the most disparaging of the Salaf of the Ummah and its imams, and the most attacking of the general body of the Ummah from all sects. So since they were farthest from following the Salaf, they were most famous for innovation. It is thus known that the hallmark of the people of innovation is abandoning the adoption of following the Salaf. Therefore Imam Ahmad said in his letter to 'Abdūs ibn Malik: 'The foundations of the Sunna with us are adherence to what the Companions of the Prophet were upon.' As for the speculative theologians of the people of affirmation (Ithbāt) from the Kullābiyya, Karrāmiyya, and Ash'ariyya, along with the jurists, Sufis, and hadith folk, these generally do not disparage the Salaf, rather they often agree with them in most of their doctrinal summaries. However, whosoever among these is more knowledgeable of hadith is more knowledgeable of the madhhab of the Salaf and more followed to it. Indeed, veneration of the Salaf is found in a group only to the extent of its adherence to the Sunna and lack of innovation. As for the idea that adopting the Salaf is a hallmark of the people of innovation, that is absolutely false; for that is not possible except where ignorance abounds and knowledge is scarce.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument from Ibn Taymiyya refuting the claim that adherence to the Salaf is a characteristic of innovators. The author begins by identifying which groups are commonly labeled as innovators among Ahl al-Sunna: the Rafidah (Shi'a) are the most famous. He notes that the common people even equate innovation with Rafd, and consider a Sunni to be anyone who is not a Rafidi. The reason for this is that the Rafidah are the most contradictory to the prophetic hadith and Quranic meanings, and the most critical of the Salaf and the general body of Muslims. Because they are furthest from following the Salaf, they are most associated with innovation. Thus, the hallmark of innovators is abandoning the way of the Salaf, not claiming to follow them. Imam Ahmad's statement is quoted to support this: the foundation of the Sunna is clinging to the path of the Companions. Next, Ibn Taymiyya discusses the speculative theologians of Ithbāt (affirmation of attributes) such as the Kullābiyya, Karrāmiyya, and Ash'ariyya, along with jurists, Sufis, and hadith scholars. These groups generally do not disparage the Salaf; they often agree with them. However, the more knowledgeable a person is in hadith, the more they understand and follow the madhhab of the Salaf. Veneration of the Salaf is proportionate to one's adherence to the Sunna and lack of innovation. The passage concludes by refuting the idea that adopting the Salaf as a slogan is a sign of innovation. That claim is absolutely false; it can only arise where ignorance is widespread and knowledge is scarce.

Key Terms
السلف
البدعة
الرافضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The Ash'ariyya
B) The Karrāmiyya
C) The Rafidah
D) The Kullābiyya

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refutes the idea that following the Salaf is a sign of innovation; instead, innovation is characterized by abandoning the Salaf, as shown by the Rafidah.

Critiquing Historical Misidentifications in Islamic Thought
Author critiques historical misconceptions about Aristotle and Alexander in relation to Islamic figures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply historical and religious criteria to evaluate and refute a false identification of figures in Islamic history.

Core Idea
Confusing historical figures undermines the integrity of Islamic narratives.

In this passage, the author corrects a serious error: the identification of Aristotle with al-Khidr and Alexander the Great with Dhul-Qarnayn. Through careful use of historical chronology and religious description, he shows why these claims are baseless. This lesson will teach you how to critically examine similar historical claims using the same method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim
First, locate the specific claim being made: that Aristotle is al-Khidr and Alexander is Dhul-Qarnayn. Note who is being equated and the basis given (if any).
The author states 'they say that Aristotle is al-Khidr' and that Alexander is Dhul-Qarnayn.
2
Examine the Historical Evidence
Look at what the author presents about the historical Alexander (son of Philip): his timeline (300 years before Christ), his extent (reached Persia only, not the Turks), and his failure to build the dam.
This directly contradicts the Quranic description of Dhul-Qarnayn.
3
Contrast with Religious Sources
Compare with the Quranic account of Dhul-Qarnayn: he reached the east and west of the earth, built the dam, and was a monotheist. Note the author's emphasis on monotheism vs. polytheism.
The author emphasizes that Dhul-Qarnayn was a 'muwahhid' while Alexander was a polytheist.
4
Note the Theological Implication
The author also points out that Aristotle and his Greek contemporaries were polytheists and practitioners of sorcery, unlike the prophetic figure al-Khidr. This religious difference is crucial.
5
Apply the Method
Generalize: to evaluate any historical misidentification, gather historical facts (chronology, geography) and compare with revealed texts (Quran, hadith) regarding the figure's attributes and actions.
This is a standard tool in Islamic historical criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هؤلاء: إنه أفلاطون أستاذ أرسطو، ويقولون: إن أرسطو هو الخَضِر (۱) . إلى أمثال هذا الكلام الذي فيه من الجهل والضلال ما لا يعلمه إلا ذو الجلال، أقل ما فيه جهلهم بتواريخ الأنبياء، فإن أرسطو باتفاقهم كان وزير الإسكندر بن فيلبس المقدوني الذي تؤرّخ له اليهود والنصارى التاريخ الرُّومي، وكان قبل المسيح بنحو ثلاثمئة سنة. وقد يظنُّون أن هذا هو ذو القرنين المذكور في القرآن، وأن أرسطو كان وزيرا لذي القرنين المذكور في القرآن (٢). وهذا جهل؛ فإن هذا الإسكندر بن فيلبس لم يصل إلى بلاد الترك، ولم يَبْنِ السَّدَّ، وإنما وصل إلى بلاد الفرس. وذو القرنين المذكورُ في القرآن وصل إلى شرق الأرض وغربها، وكان متقدما على هذا ، يقال : اسمه الإسكندر بن دارا، وكان موحدا مؤمنًا، وذاك مشركًا كان يعبد هو وقومه الكواكب والأصنام، ويُعانون السحر، كما كان أرسطو وقومه من اليونان مشركين يعبدون الأصنام ويُعَانُون السِّحر، ولهم في ذلك مصنفات، وأخبارهم مشهورة، وآثارهم ظاهرة بذلك، فأين هذا من هذا ؟!
They say: He is Plato, teacher of Aristotle, and they say that Aristotle is al-Khidr. And similar speech containing ignorance and misguidance which only the Majestic knows. The least of it is their ignorance of the histories of the prophets. For Aristotle, by their agreement, was the vizier of Alexander son of Philip the Macedonian, from whom the Jews and Christians date the Roman calendar, and he was about three hundred years before Christ. And they may think that this is Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Quran, and that Aristotle was the vizier of Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Quran. This is ignorance; for this Alexander son of Philip did not reach the lands of the Turks, did not build the dam, but only reached the lands of Persia. And Dhul-Qarnayn mentioned in the Quran reached the east and west of the earth, and was before this one. It is said his name is Alexander son of Dara, and he was a monotheist believer, while that one was a polytheist, he and his people worshipping planets and idols, and practicing sorcery, as did Aristotle and his people of the Greeks, polytheists worshipping idols and practicing sorcery. They have works on that, their reports are famous, and their traces are apparent. So how can this be compared to that?
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage is a historical critique of a false belief. The author attacks those who claim that Aristotle is al-Khidr and that Alexander the Great is the Quranic Dhul-Qarnayn. He points out that Aristotle lived 300 years before Christ and was the vizier of Alexander son of Philip, who was a polytheist, while the Quranic Dhul-Qarnayn was a monotheist who built the dam and reached both ends of the earth. The author emphasizes the historical inaccuracy and ignorance of such claims, using the well-known facts of polytheism and sorcery among the Greeks to contrast with the righteousness of Dhul-Qarnayn.

ذو القرنين (Dhul-Qarnayn)

Key Takeaway
False historical identifications can be refuted by comparing historical facts with revealed texts and noting differences in belief and action.

Ibn Taymiyya's Refutation of Batini Philosophers on Prophetic Concealment
Author explains the Batini philosophers' claim that prophets concealed true esoteric knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Batini claim about prophetic concealment and reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of its grounds and consequences.

Core Idea
The Batini doctrine turns prophets into deceivers and revelation into a tool for ignorance.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a dangerous claim: that the Prophet Muhammad knew esoteric truths but deliberately concealed them from the common people, presenting only imaginative allegories. This lesson unpacks the Batini position and Ibn Taymiyya's multi-faceted refutation, exposing the epistemological, ethical, and historical flaws in the esotericist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Batini claim about what the Prophet truly knew
Find the list of doctrines the Batini say the Prophet knew in private: God has no attributes, is not seen, does not speak; the heavens are eternal; no bodily resurrection; no angels. This is the hidden truth.
These doctrines contradict core Islamic beliefs.
2
Identify the Batini claim about why the Prophet concealed it
The Batini say the common people could not accept this truth, so the Prophet used takhyīl (imagination) and tamthīl (allegory) for their benefit, even though this involves confusion and ignorance.
This implies the Prophet was deceptive for a perceived benefit.
3
Identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the Batini imams
Ibn Taymiyya attacks the historical credibility of the Batini imams (the Fatimids), exposing their false lineage, Jewish-Magian origins, and deviation from all Islamic sects.
This undermines the authority of the esoteric tradition.
4
Synthesize the overall critique
Ibn Taymiyya uses historical, ethical, and theological arguments: the Batini claim makes the Prophet a deceiver, contradicts established Islamic doctrine, and relies on a fraudulent lineage.
The critique is a comprehensive refutation of both the idea and its proponents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا بيان ما يقوله هؤلاء الفلاسفة الباطنية فيما جاء بـه الرسول. والفريق الثاني منهم يقولون : إن الرسول كان يعلم الحقَّ الثابت في نفس الأمر في التوحيد والمعاد، ويعرفُ أن الرب ليس له صفةٌ ثبوتية، وأنه لا يرى ولا يتكلَّم، وأن الأفلاك قديمةٌ أزلية لم تزل ولا تزال، وأن الأبدان لا تعود (۱)، وأنه ليس الله ملائكة هم أحياء ناطقون ينزلون بالوحي من عنده ويصعدون إليه ولكن يقول بما عليه هؤلاء الباطنية في الباطن، لكن ما كان يمكنه إظهار ذلك للعامة؛ لأن هذا إذا ظهر لم تقبله عقولهم وقلوبهم، بل يُنْكِرون ويَنْفِرون، فأظهر لهم من التخييل والتمثيل ما ينتفعون بـه في دينهم، وإن كان في ذلك تلبيس عليهم وتجهيل لهم واعتقادهم الأمر على خلاف ما هو عليه، لما في ذلك من المصلحة لهم (۲). ويجعلون أئمة الباطنية، كبني عبيد بن ميمون القَدَّاح الذين أدعوا أنهم من ولد محمد بن إسماعيل بن جعفر، ولم يكونوا من أولاده، بل كان جدهم يهوديًا ربيبًا لمجوسي، وأظهروا التشيع، ولم يكونوا في الحقيقة على دين واحد من الشيعة لا الإمامية ولا الزيدية، بل ولا الغالية الذين يعتقدون إلهية عليّ أو نبوته، بل كانوا شرا من هؤلاء كلهم؛ ولهذا كثر تصانيف المسلمين في كشف أسرارهم وهتك أستارهم (۳)
And the intention here is to explain what these esoteric philosophers say regarding what the Messenger brought. The second group among them say: The Messenger knew the truth fixed in itself concerning tawhid and the afterlife, and knew that the Lord has no positive attribute, that He is not seen and does not speak, that the spheres are eternal and pre-eternal, never ceasing and never ending, that bodies will not return, and that there are no angels who are living, rational beings descending with revelation from Him and ascending to Him. But he said according to what these esotericists hold in private, but he could not show that to the common people; because if this were revealed, their minds and hearts would not accept it, but they would deny and flee. So he showed them from imaginative representation and allegory what they would benefit from in their religion, even if in that there is confusion for them and ignorance, and their belief in the matter opposite to what it is, due to the benefit in that for them. And they make the imams of the esotericists, like the Banu Ubayd ibn Maymun al-Qaddah who claimed they were from the descendants of Muhammad ibn Ismail ibn Ja'far, but they were not from his children; rather, their grandfather was a Jew, a stepson of a Magian, and they showed Shi'ism, but in reality they were not followers of any single school of the Shi'a, neither the Imamiyya nor the Zaydiyya, nor even the extremists who believe in the divinity or prophecy of 'Ali. Rather, they were worse than all of these; hence the Muslims' works in revealing their secrets and tearing their veils became numerous.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a critical exposition of the Batini (esoteric) philosophers' view of prophecy. The author describes how the second group of Batinis claim that the Prophet Muhammad knew the true philosophical doctrines: that God has no positive attributes, is not seen, does not speak, that the heavens are eternal, and that bodily resurrection does not occur. However, he concealed this from the common people and instead presented imaginative representations and allegories for their benefit, even though this involves deception. The author then attacks the Batini imams, specifically the dynasty of Ubayd al-Qaddah (the Fatimids), exposing their lineage as false, their Jewish-Magian origins, and their deviation from all Islamic sects, including the Shi'a. The passage highlights the author's refutation of both the doctrinal position and the historical claims of the Batinis.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة الباطنية
التخييل
التمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He did not know them himself.

D) He was forced by the angels.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the Batini view of prophetic concealment logically entails that the Prophet deceived the people, and he demolishes their historical credibility.

Critique of al-Ghazali's Philosophical Synthesis
Author describes Ghazali's philosophical inclinations and refutation by scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the grounds on which scholars critiqued al-Ghazali's philosophical tendencies and evaluate the internal validity of those critiques.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's attempt to dress philosophy in Islamic garb did not deceive the scholars.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq critiques Abu Hamid al-Ghazali for his philosophical inclinations. Even his own student Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi (likely) accused him of being trapped within philosophy. The author uses this criticism to illustrate the danger of mixing Greek thought with Islamic revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
The passage attacks al-Ghazali's philosophical leanings, not his entire legacy. Note the specific accusation: he leaned towards philosophy but disguised it in Sufi and Islamic terms.
2
Extract the grounds of critique
The critique rests on three points: (1) Ghazali's disguise of philosophy, (2) his student Abu Bakr's testimony that he could not escape philosophy, and (3) reports of Batini beliefs. Find each in the text.
3
Assess the validity of the critique
Is the critique logically sound? It relies on testimonial evidence and association with Batiniyya. Note that it does not directly refute Ghazali's arguments but attacks his method and reputation.
4
Understand the broader significance
Ibn Taymiyya includes this critique to show the danger of mixing Greek philosophy with Islamic sciences. This is part of his larger project to purify Islamic thought from foreign elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان عليه فقد خرج إلى السُّنة المحضة (١). وأبو حامد يميل إلى الفلسفة لكنه أظهرها في قالب التصوف والعبارات الإسلامية؛ ولهذا ردَّ عليه علماء المسلمين، حتى أخص أصحابه أبو بكر بن (٢) قال: «شيخنا أبو حامد دخل في بطن الفلاسفة، ثم أراد أن يخرج فما قدر»، وقد حكى عنه من القول بمذاهب الباطنية ما يوجد تصديق ذلك في كتبه، وردَّ عليه العلماء المذكورون قبل (٣).
He was upon that and went out to the pure Sunnah. And Abu Hamid leans towards philosophy but he displayed it in the mold of Sufism and Islamic expressions; because of this, the Muslim scholars refuted him, even his most special companion Abu Bakr ibn said: 'Our Shaykh Abu Hamid entered into the belly of the philosophers, then wanted to come out but could not.' And it has been reported about him of saying from the beliefs of the Batinis that which confirms that is found in his books, and the aforementioned scholars refuted him before.
Source pages p. 232

Commentary
This passage is a critical remark about Abu Hamid al-Ghazali's philosophical tendencies. The author states that Ghazali inclined towards philosophy but dressed it in Sufi and Islamic terminology. Consequently, Muslim scholars refuted him. Even his close student Abu Bakr ibn (likely Ibn al-'Arabi or another) criticized him, saying he entered the belly of philosophy and couldn't escape. The author also mentions that Ghazali is reported to have espoused Batini (esoteric) doctrines, and evidence is found in his books, and earlier scholars refuted him. The footnote numbers refer to sources, but the flow of the argument is clear: Ghazali's attempt to harmonize philosophy with Islam was seen as deceptive and rejected by orthodox scholars.

الباطنية

Detecting Inconsistency in Theological Positions
Author points out inconsistency in Ibn al-Jawzi's stance on divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate inconsistency in a scholar's position on divine attributes using the criteria Ibn Taymiyya applies here.

Core Idea
Inconsistency in affirming divine attributes undermines a scholar's entire position.

Ibn Taymiyya points out that Abu al-Faraj (Ibn al-Jawzi) wavers between two opposite positions on divine attributes: affirmation (ithbat) and negation (nafy). Worse, in his other works he affirms what he denies in the present book. This lesson trains you to spot such logical inconsistency and understand why it destroys the reliability of a theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
In this passage, the two poles are al-ithbat (affirmation of attributes) and al-nafy (negation). Locate where the author mentions both. Ibn Taymiyya says Abu al-Faraj did not remain firm on either.
These terms are central to the classical kalam debate on God's attributes.
2
Find where the scholar takes each position
Look for textual evidence: Ibn Taymiyya claims Abu al-Faraj has speech in affirmation (poetry and prose) in other works, but in this work he denies many attributes. This shows he held both positions at different times.
3
Check consistency across works
A scholar's position should be coherent across his writings. Ibn Taymiyya cross-references Abu al-Faraj's other works to show the contradiction. You can do the same by comparing a scholar's statements on the same topic in different books.
4
Assess the impact on credibility
Once inconsistency is exposed, the scholar's argument loses force. Ibn Taymiyya implies that such a wavering position cannot be trusted. Evaluate whether the inconsistency is logical (contradictory) or merely developmental (changed view over time).
Ibn Taymiyya presents it as a fatal flaw, not a legitimate evolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أن أبا الفرج نفسه متناقض في هذا الباب، لم يثبت على قدم النفي ولا على قدم الإثبات، بل له من الكلام في الإثبات نظما ونثرا ما أثبت به كثيرًا من الصفات التي أنكرها في هذا المصنف، فهو في هذا
The second aspect: that Abu al-Faraj himself is contradictory in this chapter; he does not remain firm on the foot of negation nor on the foot of affirmation. Rather, he has speech in affirmation, in poetry and prose, by which he affirmed many of the attributes that he denied in this work. So in this he is...
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a direct critique of Abu al-Faraj (Ibn al-Jawzi) for being inconsistent in his theological position on divine attributes. Ibn Taymiyya points out that in the very work under discussion, Ibn al-Jawzi wavers: he neither consistently negates (nafy) nor consistently affirms (ithbat) the attributes. Moreover, in other works of his, both in poetry and prose, he affirms many of the attributes that he denies in this particular work. This inconsistency undermines his credibility and the reliability of his attack on the author's position.

أبو الفرج
الإثبات
النفي

Key Takeaway
A scholar's inconsistency in affirming or negating divine attributes weakens the reliability of his entire theological position.

The Logical Contradiction of Mixing Madhhab and Conflicting Creed
Author argues that one cannot claim to follow an imam while disagreeing with his creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical contradiction in claiming to follow an imam in fiqh while holding a creed that the imam condemned, and explain the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
You cannot consistently follow an imam while rejecting his creed.

This passage from a critical work highlights a common inconsistency: people who claim to follow a particular madhhab in fiqh but adopt a theology that the imam himself declared deviant. The author argues that such a position is logically and religiously untenable. In this lesson, we will dissect his argument and see how it applies to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimant's position
Who is the author criticizing? Look for the examples: someone claiming Shafi'i fiqh but Ash'ari creed, or Hanbali fiqh but Mu'tazili creed.
2
Extract the imam's ruling on the opposing creed
According to the author, what did al-Shafi'i and Ahmad say about Ash'arism and Mu'tazilism? They condemned them and even declared them kufr.
3
Apply the logical principle
If an imam declared a group deviant or kafir, then claiming to follow that imam while belonging to that group is self-contradictory.
The author uses the term 'intihal' negatively to imply improper adoption.
4
Assess the author's conclusion
Is the argument valid? What basis does the author give for calling it apostasy?
The author cites the imams' own takfir of such groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ووجه ثالث لا بدَّ مِن أن نبين فيه، فنقول: إن في النقل عن هؤلاء إلزاما للحجة على كل من ينتحل مذهب إمام يخالفه في العقيدة، فإن أحدهما لا محالة يضلل صاحبه أو يبدعُه أو يكفرُه، فانتحال مذهبه مع مخالفته في العقيدة مستنكر والله شرعًا وطبعا. فمن قال: أنا شافعي الشَّرع أشعري الاعتقاد، قلنا له: هذا من الأضداد، لا بل من الارتداد؛ إذ لم يكن الشافعي أشعري الاعتقاد. ومن قال: أنا حنبلي في الفروع، معتز لي في الأصول، قلنا: قد ضللت إذا عن سواء السبيل فيما تزعمه؛ إذ لم يكن أحمد معتز لي الدين والاجتهاد. قال: وقد أفتتن أيضًا خلق من المالكية بمذاهب الأشعرية، وهذه والله شينةٌ وعار، وفلتةٌ تعود بالوبال والنكال وسوء الدار، على منتحل مذاهب هؤلاء الأئمة الكبار، فإن مذهبهم ما رويناه من تكفيرهم الجهمية والمعتزلة والقدرية والواقفية وتكفيرهم اللفظية.
He said: And a third aspect that we must explain, so we say: Indeed, in transmission from these [Salaf] there is an imposition of proof upon everyone who adopts the madhhab of an imam while opposing him in creed. For one of them inevitably declares the other astray, an innovator, or a disbeliever. So adopting his madhhab while opposing him in creed is disapproved by Allah, both in law and nature. Whoever says: 'I am Shafi'i in law, Ash'ari in creed,' we say to him: This is contradictory, nay rather apostasy; for al-Shafi'i was not Ash'ari in creed. Whoever says: 'I am Hanbali in branches, Mu'tazili in principles,' we say: You have surely gone astray from the straight path regarding what you claim; for Ahmad was not Mu'tazili in religion and ijtihad. He said: And indeed many of the Malikis have also fallen into the maddhabs of the Ash'aris, and this, by Allah, is a disgrace and shame, a blunder that brings calamity, punishment, and evil abode upon the adopters of the maddhabs of these great imams. For their madhhab is what we have narrated from them: their takfir of the Jahmis, Mu'tazilis, Qadaris, Waqifis, and their takfir of the Lafziyya.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against inconsistency in religious affiliation. The author first states that one cannot claim to follow an imam in law while contradicting his creed, because the imam would have condemned such beliefs. He provides two examples: a supposed Shafi'i who is Ash'ari (since Shafi'i opposed Ash'arism), and a supposed Hanbali who is Mu'tazili (since Ahmad opposed Mu'tazilism). He considers these positions contradictory to the point of apostasy. Then he criticizes Malikis who adopted Ash'arism, noting that the imams practiced takfir of such groups. The argument is that adopting a madhhab while rejecting the imam's 'aqida is logically and religiously untenable.

مخالفة في العقيدة
تكفير

Why Only Four Imams Are Cited
Author answers why transmission is limited to Shafi'i, Malik, Thawri, and Ahmad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's dialectical response to the objection about limited transmission and evaluate the logical sufficiency of the answer.

Core Idea
Other imams' madhhabs are subsumed or agree with the four cited.

In this passage, the author anticipates a critique: why only transmit from Shafi'i, Malik, Thawri, and Ahmad, ignoring other great imams like al-Awza'i and al-Layth? The answer is a masterclass in dialectical reasoning, using historical facts to justify a methodological choice. By breaking down the objection and its two-part reply, you will learn how a scholar defends a practice without dismissing the value of other authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Objection
Read the opening phrase: 'If it is said: Why did you not confine yourselves...' The objector challenges the author's limited selection of four imams, naming specific excluded figures (al-Awza'i, al-Layth). Note the assumption: that these imams were also followed and should be cited.
The objection is a typical munazara move: pointing out inconsistency or incompleteness.
2
Extract the Main Response
The author begins with 'We say: Because...' and offers two distinct reasons. The first reason is general: these excluded imams were 'arbab al-madhahib' (masters of madhhabs) but their madhhabs were later subsumed (indarajat) under the major ones. The second reason is specific to al-Layth: his students did not uphold his madhhab.
The response is structured as a dichotomy: subsumption (general) and lack of preservation (specific).
3
Analyze the First Reason: Subsumption (Indiraj)
The author gives the example of Ibn 'Uyayna: he was an authority but did not author a madhhab; his students (al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, Ishaq) did, so his madhhab became subsumed under theirs. This shows that citing the four imams indirectly includes the positions of teachers like Ibn 'Uyayna.
The logical force: citing the four is not exclusive but inclusive through the principle of subsumption.
4
Analyze the Second Reason: Lack of Preservation and Agreement
For al-Layth, the author notes two points: his students did not preserve his madhhab (he was 'not granted students'), and his opinions agree with Malik or al-Thawri. Thus, even if one wanted to cite him, it is sufficient to cite Malik or al-Thawri.
The second reason relies on the absence of independent transmission and substantive overlap.
5
Evaluate the Response's Dialectical Strength
Consider: Does the answer fully satisfy the objection? The objector implied that Awza'i and Layth had independent followings. The author shows that either their schools are preserved within the four (subsumption) or they lack preservation and agree with the four. This is a strong rebuttal because it addresses each named imam and provides reasons why the current practice is both sufficient and efficient.
The response is not merely defensive; it offers a positive rationale for the selection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قيل: فهلا أقتصرتم إذا على النقل عمَّن شاع مذهبه وانتُحِلَ اختياره من أصحاب الحديث، وهم الأئمة: الشافعي ومالك والثوري وأحمد، إذ لا نرى أحدا ينتحل مذهب الأوزاعي والليث وسائرهم؟ قلنا: لأن من ذكرناه من الأئمة سوى هؤلاء أرباب المذاهب في الجملة، إذ كانوا قدوةً في عصرهم، ثم أندرجت مذاهبهم بالآخرة تحت مذاهب الأئمة المعتبرة. وذلك أن ابن عيينة كان قدوة، ولكن لم يصنف في الذي كان يختاره من الأحكام، وإنما صنف أصحابه، وهم الشافعي وأحمد وإسحاق، فاندرج مذهبه تحت مذاهبهما. وأما الليث بن سعد، فلم يقم أصحابه بمذهبه، قال الشافعي: «لم يُرْزَق الأصحابَ»، إلا أن قوله يوافق قول مالك أو قول الثوري لا يخطئهما،
He said: If it is said: Why did you not confine yourselves to transmission from those whose madhhab became widespread and whose choice was adopted among the hadith scholars, namely the imams al-Shafi'i, Malik, al-Thawri, and Ahmad, since we do not see anyone adopting the madhhab of al-Awza'i, al-Layth, and the rest? We say: Because those imams we mentioned besides these were in general masters of maddhabs, as they were authorities in their time, but later their maddhabs became subsumed under the maddhabs of the recognized imams. That is because Ibn 'Uyayna was an authority, but he did not author in the rulings he chose; rather his students—al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and Ishaq—authored, so his madhhab became subsumed under theirs. As for al-Layth ibn Sa'd, his students did not uphold his madhhab; al-Shafi'i said: 'He was not granted students,' except that his opinion agrees with Malik or al-Thawri and does not differ from them.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection and answer (naqd wa jawab). The objector asks why the author only transmits from four imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Thawri, Ahmad) and not from others like Awza'i, Layth, etc. The answer provides two reasons: first, these other imams were authorities but their maddhabs were later subsumed under the major imams. For example, Ibn 'Uyayna was a leading figure but did not author a madhhab; his students (Shafi'i, Ahmad, Ishaq) did, so his madhhab is included in theirs. Second, al-Layth ibn Sa'd lacked students to preserve his madhhab; moreover, his opinions agree with Malik or Thawri. Thus, it is sufficient and more practical to rely on the four well-known imams whose maddhabs are independently preserved.

Key Terms
أرباب المذاهب
اندرجت
قدوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because his opinions contradict all other imams.

Key Takeaway
The author defends his exclusive reliance on four imams by demonstrating that other imams' legal positions are either subsumed under these four or coincide with them, making additional citation unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
